                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {

                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The complex jujube genome provides insights into fruit tree biology"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2014 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2014-10-28"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2014 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The jujube (Ziziphus jujuba Mill.), a member of family Rhamnaceae, is a major dry fruit and a traditional herbal medicine for more than one billion people. Here we present a high-quality sequence for the complex jujube genome, the first genome sequence of Rhamnaceae, using an integrated strategy. The final assembly spans 437.65&#8201;Mb (98.6% of the estimated) with 321.45&#8201;Mb anchored to the 12 pseudo-chromosomes and contains 32,808 genes. The jujube genome has undergone frequent inter-chromosome fusions and segmental duplications, but no recent whole-genome duplication. Further analyses of the jujube-specific genes and transcriptome data from 15 tissues reveal the molecular mechanisms underlying some specific properties of the jujube. Its high vitamin C content can be attributed to a unique high level expression of genes involved in both biosynthesis and regeneration. Our study provides insights into jujube-specific biology and valuable genomic resources for the improvement of Rhamnaceae plants and other fruit trees. The jujube is a major dry fruit crop in China and is commonly used for medicinal purposes. Here the authors sequence the genome and transcriptome of the most widely cultivated jujube cultivar, Dongzao, and highlight the genetic and molecular basis of agronomically important jujube traits, such as vitamin C content.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The jujube (<i>Ziziphus jujuba</i> Mill.), a member of family Rhamnaceae, is a major dry fruit and a traditional herbal medicine for more than one billion people. Here we present a high-quality sequence for the complex jujube genome, the first genome sequence of Rhamnaceae, using an integrated strategy. The final assembly spans 437.65 Mb (98.6% of the estimated) with 321.45 Mb anchored to the 12 pseudo-chromosomes and contains 32,808 genes. The jujube genome has undergone frequent inter-chromosome fusions and segmental duplications, but no recent whole-genome duplication. Further analyses of the jujube-specific genes and transcriptome data from 15 tissues reveal the molecular mechanisms underlying some specific properties of the jujube. Its high vitamin C content can be attributed to a unique high level expression of genes involved in both biosynthesis and regeneration. Our study provides insights into jujube-specific biology and valuable genomic resources for the improvement of Rhamnaceae plants and other fruit trees.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The jujube (<i>Ziziphus jujuba</i> Mill.) is the most economically important member of the Rhamnaceae, a large cosmopolitan family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="James, E. R., Michael, F. F., Quentin, C. B. C., Diane, B. &amp; Mark, W. C. A phylogenetic analysis of Rhamnaceae using rbcL and trnL-F plastid DNA sequences. Am. J. Bot. 87, 1309–1324 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13381e799">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chen, Y. &amp; Schirarend, C. Flora of China, 12, (Science Press (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13381e802">2</a></sup>. It is one of the oldest cultivated fruit trees in the world, with evidence of domestication dating back to 7,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Qu, Z. &amp; Wang, Y. Chinese Fruit Trees Record-Chinese Jujube China Forestry Publishing House (1993)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13381e806">3</a></sup>. It is native to China and is now a major dry fruit crop with a cultivation area of 2 million ha, the main source of income for 20 million farmers as well as a traditional herbal medicine for more than one billion people in Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Liu, M. China Jujube Development Report,1949–2007 China Forestry Publishing House (2008)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13381e810">4</a></sup>. It has been introduced into at least 47 countries from temperate to tropical zones throughout the five continents and is becoming increasingly popular worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Liu, M., Liu, P. &amp; Liu, G. Advances of research on germplasm resources of Chinese jujube. Acta Hortic. 993, 15–20 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d13381e814">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Liu, M. Chinese jujube: botany and horticulture. Hortic. Rev. 32, 229–298 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d13381e817">6</a></sup>.</p><p>The jujube has a range of botanical and horticultural features<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Liu, M. Chinese jujube: botany and horticulture. Hortic. Rev. 32, 229–298 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d13381e824">6</a></sup> that gives it great potential in fruit tree molecular improvement, human health protection, and the economical development and ecological restoration of arid region. It is well-adapted to various biotic and abiotic stresses, especially drought and salinity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 1</a>), and is considered an ideal cash crop for arid and semi-arid areas where common fruits and grain/oil crops do not grow well. Its fruit is an excellent source of vitamin C (higher than the well-known vitamin C-rich orange and kiwifruit) and sugar (25–30%, twice as high as most common fruits and even higher than sugarcane and sugar beet)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Y., Wang, G. &amp; Pan, X. China Food Composition-Book1, 2nd edn Peking Univ. Medical Press (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d13381e831">7</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The jujube also has a very easy and quick flower bud differentiation (only ~7 days), a long flowering season lasting for 2 months, a very short period of ~6 months from planting or sowing to yielding fruits, and a very long lifecycle, even more than 1,000 productive years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Qu, Z. &amp; Wang, Y. Chinese Fruit Trees Record-Chinese Jujube China Forestry Publishing House (1993)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13381e838">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Liu, M. Chinese jujube: botany and horticulture. Hortic. Rev. 32, 229–298 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d13381e841">6</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><p>Furthermore, jujube tree has evolved a distinct self-shoot-pruning system comprising four types of shoot namely primary shoot, secondary shoot, mother bearing shoot (MBS) and bearing shoot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Liu, M. Chinese jujube: botany and horticulture. Hortic. Rev. 32, 229–298 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d13381e852">6</a></sup>, each of which has a very different function and developmental pattern. Primary shoot is the only normally extended shoot. Secondary shoot occurs from each node of the primary shoot and its tip dies back naturally. MBS is the branch producing bearing shoots, it is formed at each node of the secondary shoot and is extremely condensed elongating only ~1 mm per year. Bearing shoot is the only fruiting shoot, it is deciduous and drops before winter normally, which is a very uncommon trait in tree plants. This self-shoot-pruning system makes it easy to control tree size, and the diversified shoot types offers a unique model for elucidating shoot development and function.</p><p>Sugar and vitamin C contents are the most common indicators of fruit quality, pruning is the most labour-consuming work of orchard management, earlier fruiting and more productive years are what farmers expect, and drought and salinity are the main abiotic stresses for fruit growing. Therefore, the aforementioned properties of the jujube are of great importance to the modern fruit production characterized by fast payback, easy management and labour-saving. In addition, the jujube is a close relative of Rosaceae (both belonging to the Rosales order in the widely accepted molecular taxonomy system of Angiosperm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="The Angiosperm Phylogeny Group. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG III. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 161, 105–121 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13381e859">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, S., Solitis, D. E., Yang, Y., Li, D. &amp; Yi, T. Multi-gene analysis provides a well-supported phylogeny of rosales. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 60, 21–28 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d13381e862">9</a></sup>), the most important fruit-producing family containing a large number of leading deciduous fruit species such as apple (<i>Malus domestica</i>), pear (<i>Pyrus bretschneideri</i>), peach (<i>Prunus persica</i>), strawberry (<i>Fragaria vesca</i>) and <i>Rubus</i>. Consequently, the jujube could be a rich source of genes for the molecular improvement of fruit trees, and a fundamental understanding of the genetics of the jujube is crucial.</p><p>So far, more than 70 plant genomes have been sequenced and assembled since the genome sequence of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 794–815 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13381e888">10</a></sup> was published in 2000. However, high level of heterozygosity and repeated sequences and low content of GC are still the main obstacles for genome sequencing and assembly using the next-generation-sequencing (NGS) technology. Owing to the short read length of the NGS technology, the assembling algorithm is always based on de Bruijn graph<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13381e892">11</a></sup>, where heterozygous locus between haploid becomes a bubble resulting in the breakdown of the final assembly at the heterozygous locus. The repeated sequences make the assembly fragmental in the similar way. Bacterial artificial chromosome-to-bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC-to-BAC) strategy was reintroduced and a few genomes have been assembled at a reasonable level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhang, G. et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature 490, 49–54 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13381e896">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="You, M. et al. A heterozygous moth genome provides insights into herbivory and detoxification. Nat. Genet. 45, 220–225 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13381e899">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wu, J. et al. The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri Rehd.). Genome Res. 23, 396–408 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13381e902">14</a></sup>. We revealed not only both high heterozygosity and high density of repeated sequence but also low GC content in the jujube genome, which indicated that new method should be applied to obtain a good quality sequence for this complex genome.</p><p>Knowledge of jujube genetics and genomics is very limited, and no genome-wide study (data on the genome size, heterozygosity and a completed molecular genetic linkage map) on any members of the family Rhamnaceae has so far been published, which has significantly hindered the molecular breeding, biological research and deep utilization of the jujube. In this research, we generate and analyze a high-quality genome sequence of one of the oldest and most widely cultivated Chinese jujube cultivars, ‘Dongzao’ (2<i>n</i>=2<i>x</i>=24), using a novel strategy integrating whole-genome shotgun (WGS) sequencing, BAC-to-BAC and a PCR-free library. We also conduct comprehensive transcriptome analyses of 15 different tissues and evolutionary comparisons with related species to identify genetic characteristics that are likely to underpin some of the most valuable traits of jujube. Our study offers a rich resource of genetic information for the breeding of jujube and the molecular improvement of other Rhamnaceae plants and fruit species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly of the complex jujube genome</h3><p>Our analysis of the 17-mer frequency distribution based on short insert size (&lt;1 Kbp) clean data and simulation revealed a heterozygosity of 1.90% in the jujube genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). This is the highest degree of heterozygosity among the plants sequenced to date by NGS technology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 4</a>), which is almost twice the second-highest level of heterozygosity in plants (1.02%, pear)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wu, J. et al. The genome of the pear (Pyrus bretschneideri Rehd.). Genome Res. 23, 396–408 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13381e936">14</a></sup>. In addition, the density of simple sequence repeats (SSRs) in the jujube genome reached 378.1 SSRs per Mb, which is 2.00 times and 2.84 times that found in its close relative species peach and apple, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Tables 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">6</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="The International Peach Genome Initiative. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 487–494 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13381e947">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Velasco, R. et al. The genome of the domesticated apple (Malus x domestica Borkh.). Nat. Genet. 42, 833–839 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d13381e950">16</a></sup>. The very high level of heterozygosity, very high density of SSRs and low GC content (33.41%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Tab1">Table 1</a>) make the jujube genome a challenge for the WGS strategy using NGS technology.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of assembly and annotation for the jujube genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms6315/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A strategy combining WGS sequencing, BAC-to-BAC and WGS-PCR-free library was employed to reduce the impact of the complexity of the jujube genome. WGS was used to construct libraries of different insert sizes, ranging from 170 bp to 40 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 7</a>). A total of 21,504 BAC clones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 8</a>) were selected for sequencing at an average of 68 × coverage using the Illumina HiSeq 2000 system, representing a total of 5.86 × coverage of jujube genome size. These BAC sequences were taken as BAC end data to overcome the heterozygosity problem and to improve genome assembly. Paired-end-libraries of 170 to 800 bp were constructed and sequenced at 318 × coverage to fill in the gaps. WGS mate-pair libraries of 2, 5, 10, 20 and 40 kb were constructed and sequenced at 69 × coverage to build super scaffolds. However, parts of the genome were missing from the sequencing data owing to low GC content and the high density and even distribution of SSRs. Thus, WGS-PCR-free libraries were constructed and sequenced to reduce the bias in library construction caused by low GC content genome regions, then 30 Mb sequences were added to the final assembly. Finally, we assembled all the above sequencing data into 28,930 contigs and 5,898 scaffolds with N50 sizes of 33.95 kb and 301.04 kb, respectively, spanning 437.65 Mb of the genome sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 9</a>). The assembly covered 98.6% of the jujube genome (444 Mb) estimated by our 17-mer sequence analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). In jujube genome, the percentage of low GC content sequences (with GC% range from 25–30%) are much higher than that in grape vine and peach genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><p>To further evaluate the accuracy of the genome assembly, full-length sequences of four randomly selected BACs, later demonstrated to be located in pseudo-chromosomes 1, 2, 3 and 11 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>), were sequenced and assembled using Sanger sequencing technology. Each BAC was aligned to three scaffolds at most, with an average coverage ratio of 98.5% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). In addition, our assembled sequence covered &gt;98.1% of the 1,942 published ESTs (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucest/?term%20=jujube">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucest/?term=jujube</a>, downloaded on July 2013) and 97.8% of the transcriptome sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Tables 11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">12</a>). Together, the above results indicate the high quality of our jujube genome sequence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome sequence anchoring and pseudo-chromosome construction</h3><p>To anchor the assembled genome sequences to the jujube chromosomes, we mapped the scaffolds to a high density molecular genetic map that we constructed <i>de novo</i> using an inter-specific population (105 progenies) between <i>Z. jujuba</i> (female, <i>2n</i>=2<i>x</i>=24) and <i>Z. acidojujuba</i> (male, <i>2n</i>=2<i>x</i>=24). Each individual in the F1 population was genotyped by restriction site-associated DNA sequencing (RAD-Seq). Sequencing reads of parents and the population are aligned to the jujube genome sequences using SOAP2 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 13</a>). The final map spanned 974.01 cM for the female parent and 935.40 cM for the male parent across the 12 linkage groups, with a mean genetic distance between markers (single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs)) of 0.56 cM and 0.43 cM, respectively. The joint map across the 12 linkage groups was composed of 2,419 SNP markers spanning 1,020.22 cM, with a mean marker distance of 0.42 cM.</p><p>Combining the assembled genome sequences and the genetic linkage groups, we constructed pseudo-molecules for each of the 12 chromosomes and ordered them on the basis of genetic length (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Using the mapped markers with known sequences that were uniquely aligned to the assembled scaffolds, a total of 1,120 scaffolds were anchored to the 12 linkage groups, comprising 73.56% (321.45 Mb) of the jujube genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Of the anchored scaffolds, 784 could be oriented (267.98 Mb, 83.37% of the total anchored sequences), suggesting high alignment accordance between the anchored genetic markers and the sequenced scaffolds.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Alignment of the genome sequence assembly with the genetic map of jujube."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Alignment of the genome sequence assembly with the genetic map of jujube.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="453" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>In total, 1,120 assembled scaffolds (blue) were anchored in the 12 linkage groups (LG1–LG12, red) using 2,213 corresponding SNP genetic markers (grey bars). The LG numbers were assigned on the basis of the estimated length of the genetic linkage groups.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome annotation and characterization</h3><p>We annotated the jujube genome by combining <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions, protein-based homology searches and experimental data (RNA-Seq). A total of 32,808 protein-coding genes with an average coding sequence length of 1,190 bp and 4.5 exons per gene were predicted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Tables 14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">15</a>). Overall, 78.26% of the gene models were predicted to contain two or more exons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 16</a>), and 89.80% of the predicted proteins were supported by the RNA-Seq data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 17</a>), showing the high accuracy of gene annotation. In addition, we identified a total of 410 ribosomal RNAs, 1,209 transfer RNAs, 286 small nuclear RNAs and 272 microRNAs in the jujube genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 18</a>).</p><p>Tissue-specific genes and housekeeping genes were also screened in our 24 Gb of RNA-Seq data from five tissues. We found that 613, 249, 382, 553 and 184 genes were specifically expressed in the root, shoot, leaf, flower or fruit, respectively. The 16,478 genes shared by all the five tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>) accounted for 81.43% of the total recorded and 50.22% of the total predicted protein-coding genes in the jujube genome. Among these shared genes, 1,818 were constitutively expressed housekeeping genes (<i>τ</i>&lt;0.07, based on the tissue specificity index<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Yanai, I. et al. Genome-wide midrange transcription profiles reveal expression level relationships in human tissue specification. Bioinformatics 21, 650–659 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1405">17</a></sup>), including 102 encoding ribosomal proteins and 21 encoding translation initiation factors (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Data 1</a>).</p><p>We next characterized the genome by investigating genome duplication and GC content, gene density, repeat sequences, and SSR and SNP distribution along the pseudo-chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). We identified 4.77 million SNPs in our jujube genome sequence. In total, 79.55% of them were anchored on the 12 pseudo-chromosomes, and the overall polymorphism density was 11 SNPs per kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 19</a>). The SSRs were not only present at a high density (378.1 SSRs per Mb), they were also fairly evenly distributed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, vi). We observed large regions that alternate between high and low gene density (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, iv), which was also noted in peach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="The International Peach Genome Initiative. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 487–494 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1427">15</a></sup>. The density of repeated sequences also reflects gene density (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, iv and v). The GC content is distributed unevenly in most pseudo-chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, iii).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Characterization of the jujube genome, and duplication and synteny among jujube, pear and P. mume."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Characterization of the jujube genome, and duplication and synteny among jujube, pear and <i>P. mume</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="531" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The genomic landscape of the 12 jujube pseudo-chromosomes. All density information was counted in non-overlapping 200-kb windows. i, synteny relationship of gene blocks between pseudo-chromosomes; ii, ideograms of the 12 pseudo-chromosomes; iii, GC content density; iv, gene density; v, repeat density, estimated by counting the repeat numbers of non-redundant repeat annotation results; vi, SSR density and vii, SNP density. (<b>b</b>) 4DTv (fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion) value between syntenic gene pairs among the jujube, pear and <i>P. mume</i>. (<b>c</b>) Schematic representation of syntenies among the jujube (pseudo-chromosomes 1–12), <i>P. mume</i> (chromosomes 1–8) and pear genomes (chromosomes 1–17). Each line represents a syntenic region.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified a total of 216.6 Mb of repeated sequences (49.49% of the assembled genome) in the jujube genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 20</a>). Among these sequences, 94.6% are transposable elements (TEs). The majority of TEs are retrotransposons (38.03% of the genome), whereas DNA transposons account for 8.08% of the genome. The most abundant (64%) retrotransposons are long-terminal repeat elements, of which 37% are Gypsy-type elements and 27% are of the Copia type (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 21</a>). TE content in jujube genome (46.82%), compared with its close relatives, appears to be similar to that in mulberry (47%; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="He, N. et al. Draft genome sequence of the mulberry tree Morus notabilis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2445 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1485">18</a>), and higher than that in peach (37%; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="The International Peach Genome Initiative. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 487–494 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1488">15</a>) and <i>P. mume</i> (45%; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhang, Q. et al. The genome of Prunus mume. Nat. Commun. 3, 1318 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1495">19</a>). About 99.41% of TEs had a divergence rate of &gt;10% (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>), indicating that most jujube TEs are relatively ancient<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="He, N. et al. Draft genome sequence of the mulberry tree Morus notabilis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2445 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1502">18</a></sup>.</p><p>In total, 23,996 genes (73% of the total number of annotated genes) were allocated on the 12 pseudo-chromosomes. Self-alignment of the jujube genome sequences based on the 23,996 gene models identified 943 paralogous gene groups (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2a,i</a>), indicating that the jujube genome may have undergone frequent inter-chromosome fusions and segmental duplication during its evolutionary history. Interestingly, the gene blocks located in the region from 9.20–14.68 Mb of pseudo-chromosome 1 showed obvious synteny with all other chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>), while the very low SNP density in this block (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, vii) suggests that it is highly conserved. Further analysis revealed that this block contains many genes relating to sugar metabolism and stress tolerance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Data 2</a>). Consequently, it might be crucial for understanding the unusual characteristics of jujube.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome comparison with closely related species</h3><p>To estimate speciation times, we constructed a phylogenetic tree based on single-copy genes of jujube and the seven other sequenced species of Rosales, with <i>A. thaliana</i> (Brassicaceae) as the outgroup (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). The results suggest a speciation time of 79.9 million years ago (Mya) for jujube and the clades of mulberry (<i>Morus alba</i>, Moraceae) and <i>Cannabis sativa</i> (Cannabaceae), 87.2 Mya for jujube and Rosaceae (including <i>P. bretschneideri</i>, <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, <i>P. persica</i> and <i>F. vesca</i>), and 108.5 Mya for jujube and <i>A. thaliana</i>. The speciation time is consistent with the origin time inferred from the fossil records and the recent molecular estimation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Bell, C. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The age and diversification of the angiosperms re-revisited. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1296–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1561">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Magallón, S. A. Flowering plants (Magnoliophyta). inThe Timetree of Life eds Hedges S. B., Kumar S. Oxford Univ. Press (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1564">21</a></sup>. Moreover, our data showed that jujube and the clade of <i>M. alba</i> and <i>C. sativa</i> diverged much earlier than the divergence of <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>F. vesca</i> in Rosaceae. Consequently, the jujube is the most ancient species among all of the sequenced species of Rosales (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). This result supports that Rhamnaceae has a long history and can be traced back to the Campanian<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Calvillo-Canadel, L. &amp; Sergio, R. S. Cevallos-Ferriz. Reproductive structures of Rhamnaceae from the Cerro Del Pueblo (Late Cretaceous, Coahuila) and Coatzingo (Oligocene, Puebla) Formations, Mexico. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1658–1669 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1584">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Richardson, J. E. et al. A revision of the tribal classification of Rhamnaceae. Kew Bull. 55, 311–340 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1587">23</a></sup>. Thus, our analysis provides new insights into the phylogeny of Rosales plants on the basis of genome-scale data.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Estimation of divergence time between Z. jujuba and other sequenced Rosales species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Estimation of divergence time between <i>Z. jujuba</i> and other sequenced Rosales species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="337" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The blue numbers on the nodes are the divergence times from the present (in million years ago). The calibration time of the divergence of the malvids and fabids (108–109 million years ago) is derived from He <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="He, N. et al. Draft genome sequence of the mulberry tree Morus notabilis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2445 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d13381e1611">18</a></sup></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further analyze the evolutionary divergence of jujube and other species, fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion (4DTv) rates were calculated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The 4DTv value peaked at only 0.50 in jujube, suggesting no recent whole-genome duplication occurred. The 4DTv value peaked at 0.06 and 0.50 for paralog pairs in pear, highlighting the recent whole-genome duplication and α and β duplication in this species. The orthologs between jujube and pear and between jujube and <i>P. mume</i> showed that 4DTv value peaked at 0.32 and 0.27, respectively, indicating that the divergence time between jujube and pear was earlier. All 4DTv values among paralogs in jujube, pear and <i>P. mume</i> peaked at ~0.50, indicating that the hexaploidy in these eudicots occurred at a similar time and before the split of Rhamnaceae and Rosaceae.</p><p>To study the evolutionary events leading to the modern genome structure of the jujube, we analyzed the syntenic relationships among jujube, pear and <i>P. mume</i> (all belong to order Rosales). About 4,954 (jujube versus pear) and 5,722 (jujube versus <i>P. mume</i>) orthologous genes in the jujube genome were selected. We then investigated the detailed orthologous chromosome-to-chromosome relationships between these species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). The complicated syntenic patterns, illustrated as mosaic chromosome-to-chromosome orthologous relationships, unveiled a high degree of chromosomal evolution and rearrangements among these three species. Even so, clear syntenies could be seen among chromosomes of the three species, such as pseudo-chromosome 12 of jujube, chromosome 4 of <i>P. mume</i> and chromosome 17 of pear. The analysis of the synteny blocks among jujube, strawberry (<i>F. vesca</i>), peach (<i>P. persica</i>) and grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>) indicated that the jujube genome shares high collinearity with strawberry and peach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 22</a>).</p><p>To better understand jujube-specific biology, we performed comparative analyses between the jujube genome and the other six sequenced species of Rosales (apple, pear, peach, <i>P. mume</i>, strawberry and mulberry). We then screened these genomes based on annotated genes in the public database. Of the 32,808 protein-coding genes (13,843 gene families) in the jujube genome, 1,043 gene families are specific to jujube, and the largest number of gene clusters (11,930 families) is shared with peach (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>), indicating a closer relationship between jujube and peach than between jujube and the other species analyzed.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Jujube-specific genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Jujube-specific genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="355" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Venn diagram of orthologous gene families. Seven closely related Rosales species (jujube, apple, pear, strawberry, peach, <i>P. mume</i> and mulberry) were used to generate the Venn diagram based on the gene family cluster analysis. The numbers in parentheses are gene families in jujube compared with all the other six related species, and the numbers without parentheses are those compared with the four species shown in the figure. (<b>b</b>) Ratios and distributions of jujube-specific gene sets (expansion-related genes, genes under positive selection and unique genes) in KEGG pathways. I, biosynthesis of other secondary metabolites; II, lipid metabolism; III, ascorbate and aldarate metabolism; IV, carbohydrate metabolism; V, glycan biosynthesis and metabolism; VI, amino acid metabolism; VII, nucleotide metabolism; VIII, energy metabolism; IX, metabolism of terpenoids and polyketides; X, signal transduction; XI, environmental adaptation; XII, cellular processes and XIII, genetic information processing.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Expanded gene families, unique genes and genes under positive selection have important roles in plant development and evolution. The jujube-specific gene sets compared with apple, pear, strawberry, peach, <i>P. mume</i> and mulberry were analyzed based on the KEGG orthology pathway database, to give an insight into jujube-specific biology and adaptation. A total of 2,791 unique genes, 254 genes under positive selection and 39 expanded gene families (2,650 genes) were found and assigned to KEGG pathways (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). A further functional characterization of the above genes revealed that most of them are involved in energy metabolism, vitamin C metabolism, sugar-related metabolism and secondary metabolism. This result is consistent with the special physiological characteristics of the jujube.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genes involved in extreme accumulation of vitamin C in fruit</h3><p>To explore the molecular mechanism underlying the high content of vitamin C (or ascorbic acid (AsA)) in jujube fruit, we analyzed the genes encoding the key enzymes involved in all the four known AsA biosynthesis pathways and the recycling pathway during jujube fruit development. We found that those genes are involved in two of the four pathways and most of them are specific to L-galactose pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a> red part). Much fewer genes are associated with myo-inositol pathway, suggesting which maybe a compensatory pathway in jujube (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a> blue part). Expression analyses showed that GDP-D-mannose 3,5-epimerase and GDP-L-galactose phosphorylase (two key enzymes in the <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-galactose biosynthesis pathway) and monodehydroascorbate reductase (<i>MDHAR</i>, the key enzyme in the AsA recycling pathway) were expressed at continuously higher levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genes involved in sugar and AsA metabolism."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Genes involved in sugar and AsA metabolism.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="896" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The pathway involved in AsA metabolism. (<b>b</b>) Heat map of RNA-Seq data for genes involved in AsA and sugar-related metabolism during jujube fruit ripening. <i>Y</i>, young fruit; <i>WM</i>, white mature fruit; <i>HR</i>, half-red fruit; <i>FR</i>, full red fruit. Scaled log2 expression values are shown from green to red, indicating low to high expression. <i>GME</i>, GDP-D-mannose 3′,5′-epimerase; <i>GGP</i>, GDP-L-galactose phosphorylase; <i>VTC4</i>, inositol-phosphate phosphatase/<span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-galactose 1-phosphate phosphatase; <i>TAD</i>, <span class="u-small-caps">D</span>-threo-aldose 1-dehydrogenase; <i>Ga1LDH</i>, <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-galactono-1,4-lactone dehydrogenase; <i>MDHAR</i>, monodehydroascorbate reductase (NADH); <i>AO</i>, <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-ascorbate oxidase; <i>AP</i>, <span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-ascorbate peroxidase; <i>MIOX</i>, inositol oxygenase; <i>XET</i>, xyloglucan:xyloglucosyl transferase; <i>PE</i>, pectinesterase; <i>BGluc</i>, beta-glucosidase; <i>BG2</i>, beta-1,3-glucanase 2; <i>GALE</i>, UDP-glucose 4-epimerase; <i>SPS</i>, sucrose-phosphate synthase; <i>SUS2</i>, sucrose synthase; FBA, fructose-bisphosphate aldolase; <i>GFS</i>, GDP-<span class="u-small-caps">L</span>-fucose synthase; <i>PFP</i>, pyrophosphate-fructose-6-phosphate 1-phosphotransferase; <i>PGM</i>, 2,3-bisphosphoglycerate-dependent phosphoglycerate mutase; <i>PDC</i>, pyruvate decarboxylase; <i>PGK</i>, phosphoglycerate kinase; <i>ALDH</i>, aldehyde dehydrogenase family 7 member A1; <i>OT</i>, oligosaccharyltransferase complex subunit gamma; <i>RFS</i>, raffinose synthase; <i>NRS/ER</i>, 3,5-epimerase/4-reductase; <i>AXS2</i>, UDP-apiose/xylose synthase; <i>UGT</i>, UDP-glucosyltransferase; <i>CesA</i>, cellulose synthase A; <i>MFS</i>, MFS transporter. (<b>c</b>) Upper part, phylogenetic analysis of the <i>MDHAR</i> gene family in jujube and seven related species; Lower part, the location of eight MDHAR genes in two scaffolds.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparisons with the six other sequenced Rosales species at the genome level revealed that GDP-L-galactose phosphorylase is a positively selected gene and that <i>MDHAR</i> exhibits significant expansion in the jujube genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5a,c</a>). Based on the phylogenetic analysis of seven Rosales species (including jujube) and one AsA-rich species (sweet orange, <i>Citrus sinensis</i>) of the order Sapindales, we found that there are five major <i>MDHAR</i> gene subfamilies, of which subfamilies V and IV are specific to jujube and Rosaceae species, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>). There are eight copies in subfamily V, which are adjacently located on two scaffolds (six in No. 402 and two in No. 627, No. 402 positioned to pseudo-chromosome 1) in the jujube genome and likely to have been generated by a tandem duplication. Interestingly, there is only one <i>MDHAR</i> copy (subfamilies I, II and III) in the species without recent whole-genome duplication including <i>F. vesca</i> (strawberry), <i>P. persica</i> (peach), <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M. alba</i> (mulberry), <i>Z. jujuba</i> (jujube) and <i>C. sinensis</i> (sweet orange), but there are two or more copies in the species that have undergone recent whole-genome duplication, that is, <i>M. domestica</i> (apple) and <i>P. bretschneideri</i> (pear).</p><p>Collectively, our data indicate that the L-galactose pathway is the major route to AsA biosynthesis and that <i>MDHAR</i> contributes to AsA regeneration in jujube.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genes involved in high level accumulation of sugar in fruit</h3><p>To investigate the molecular mechanism underlying the high content of sugar in jujube fruit, we analyzed the genomic and RNA-Seq data and the sugar contents of fruits at four crucial development stages (young, white mature, half-red and full red).</p><p>Our analysis showed that, fructose and glucose are the predominant sugars in young jujube fruits, whereas in mature fruits, sucrose and total sugar content are substantially increased, with sucrose becoming the dominant sugar (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 23</a>). The annotated jujube genome contains 393 genes involved in starch and sucrose metabolism, 98 involved in galactose metabolism, 67 involved in fructose and mannose metabolism, and 195 involved in amino sugar and nucleotide sugar metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Data 3</a>). Compared with other sequenced species of the order Rosales, all these gene families are expanded in jujube to some extent (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>, IV and V), which is consistent with the high expression patterns of these genes in jujube fruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>). The final sugar accumulation is determined by sugar being transported from the phloem into the fruit. We found that the expression of most genes related to the major facilitator superfamily sugar transporter was enhanced in parallel with jujube fruit ripening (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genes involved in the distinct self-shoot-pruning system</h3><p>We generated and analyzed RNA-Seq data on the four types of shoot to investigate the genetic basis underlying jujube shoot biology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>). Among the four types of shoots, the number of genes differentially expressed is the smallest between primary shoot and secondary shoot, which indicates the similar metabolism of the two predominant shoots in vegetative growth. The genes related to secondary metabolism, including those involved in arginine, lipid and polyamine metabolism, were expressed at much higher levels in these two types of shoot than in bearing shoot. These results are consistent with the environmental suitability of different types of shoot.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Heat map of the normalized RNA-Seq data for genes involved in shoot development."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Heat map of the normalized RNA-Seq data for genes involved in shoot development.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms6315/MediaObjects/41467_2014_Article_BFncomms6315_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="583" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><i>P</i>, primary shoot; <i>S</i>, secondary shoot; <i>M</i>, mother bearing shoot; <i>B</i>, bearing shoot. Scaled log2 expression values are shown from green to red, indicating low to high expression, respectively. <i>LHC</i>, light-harvesting complex; <i>FDA</i>, fructose-bisphosphate aldolase; <i>MATE</i>, multidrug resistance protein; <i>CTK</i>, cytokinin receptor; <i>MYC2</i>, transcription factor MYC2; <i>SRK2</i>, serine/threonine-protein kinase SRK2; <i>CNS</i>, chitinase; <i>JAZ</i>, jasmonate ZIM domain-containing protein; <i>CDPK</i>, calcium-dependent protein kinase; <i>GID</i>, gibberellin receptor; <i>BRI</i>, protein brassinosteroid insensitive; <i>DELLA</i>, DELLA protein; <i>IAA</i>, auxin-responsive protein; <i>PYR/PYL</i>, abscisic acid receptor PYR/PYL family; <i>SAGT</i>, pathogen-inducible salicylic acid glucosyltransferase; <i>ETR</i>, ethylene receptor; <i>PP2C</i>, protein phosphatase 2C; <i>CTR1</i>, serine/threonine-protein kinase CTR1; <i>JAR</i>, jasmonic acid-amino synthetase; <i>UGT</i>, UDP-glucosyltransferase; <i>CA</i>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>-transporting ATPase; <i>SAUR</i>, SAUR family protein; <i>WRKY</i>, WRKY transcription factor; <i>CYCD3</i>, cyclin D3; ARR-A, two-component response regulator; <i>FLS2</i>, LRR receptor-like serine/threonine-protein kinase FLS2; <i>BG</i>, beta-glucosidase. The figures on the right indicate different types of shoot.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms6315/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In MBS, the genes involved in abscisic acid (ABA) synthesis, such as the <i>PYR/PYL</i> genes, were much more highly expressed than in other shoot types, which might be a genetic basis of the extreme slow growth of MBS and the deciduous habit of bearing shoot attached on MBS. And the genes involved in porphyrin and chlorophyll metabolism were dramatically downregulated in the MBS, illustrating the decline in photosynthesis in this type of shoot.</p><p>In bearing shoot, the genes related to photosynthesis, such as those in the light-harvesting chlorophyll binding protein gene family and those involved in carbohydrate metabolism, were expressed at much higher levels than in all the other three shoot types, and the genes responsive to brassinosteroids and cytokinins were expressed at much lower levels in bearing shoots. The gene expression profiles meet the needs of its fruiting function and deciduous habit.</p><p>One of the more interesting things is that bearing shoots are normally deciduous, but could become lignified and persistent (not drop in the winter) in case they are extremely vigorous (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). The genes involved in plant hormone signal transduction were expressed in patterns, suggesting that they play key roles in this process. Small auxin-up RNA, cyclin D3 and two-component response regulator ARR-A family, which are responsive to auxin, brassinosteroids and cytokinins, respectively, were much more highly expressed in lignified bearing shoots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 24</a>). However, genes responsive to ethylene and ABA, such as ABA responsive element binding factor, serine/threonine-protein kinase SRK2 and ethylene-responsive transcription factor 1, were more strongly expressed in deciduous bearing shoots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 24</a>). In addition, genes responsive to jasmonic acid were expressed at lower levels in deciduous bearing shoots than in persistently lignified ones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 24</a>), indicating weaker stress resistance of the deciduous bearing shoot.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genes involved in the adaptation to abiotic/biotic stress</h3><p>Gene Ontology annotation of the jujube-specific gene families showed that some of them may take part in defence and stress responses (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>, XI and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Data 4</a>). The number of the gene families functionally annotated as ‘response to stress’ reaches up to 854. Arginine metabolism plays an important role in plants’ perception and adaptation to environmental disturbances, and many jujube-specific genes are also enriched in this pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>, VI).</p><p>The genes responding to osmotic stress are expressed at very high levels at all stages of fruit development, which is consistent with jujube's salt tolerance and drought resistance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 25</a>). Chitinases are pathogenesis related proteins that are induced by biotic and abiotic stress in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bishop, J. G., Dean, A. M. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Rapid evolution in plant chitinases: molecular targets of selection in plant-pathogen coevolution. PNAS 97, 5322–5327 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2144">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Punja, Z. K. &amp; Zhang, Y. Plant chitinases and their roles in resistance to fungal diseases. J. Nematol. 25, 526–540 (1993)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2147">25</a></sup>. The high expression of chitinases in jujube shoots may contribute to its high stress tolerance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 26</a>).</p><p>There are 13 genes encoding homologues of autophagy-related protein 9 in the jujube genome, and only 1–2 in the other 6 related species of Rosales (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 27</a>). Autophagy has important roles in various cellular functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Levine, B. &amp; Klionsky, D. J. Development by self-digestion: molecular mechanisms and biological functions of autophagy. Dev. Cell 6, 463–477 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2160">26</a></sup>. Specifically, in immunity, the stimulation of autophagy in infected cells helps the cells to degrade and eliminate intracellular pathogens. Therefore, autophagy may play important roles in jujube's defence responses.</p><p>Resistance (R) proteins function mainly in biotic stress responses, and they possess both a nucleotide-binding site (NBS) and a leucine rich repeat (LRR). Enrichment analyses of both IntrePro domains and gene ontology terms showed that the 849 <i>R</i> genes in jujube could be classified into 7 groups: CC-NBS-LRR, CC-NBS, LRR-RLK, NBS-LRR, NBS, TIR-NBS-LRR and TIR-NBS. The CC-NBS-LRR group (115 genes) was the most abundant among the 11 sequenced species from diversified taxa (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 28</a>). A large number of <i>R</i> genes (140, 16%) are scattered throughout chromosome 9, indicating that this chromosome is an important candidate target for further research on disease resistance of jujube. Moreover, 17% of jujube genes with a nucleotide-binding adaptor shared by APAF-1, R proteins and CED-4 domain are unique genes or positively selected genes, which suggests that the function of disease resistance has been reinforced during jujube evolution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 29</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>High rate of heterozygosity remains a particular challenge for <i>de novo</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Schatz, M. C., Witkowski, J. &amp; McCombie, W. R. Current challenges in de novo plant genome sequencing and assembly. Genome Biol. 13, 243 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2191">27</a></sup>. However, most perennial tree species are highly heterozygous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Miller, A. J. &amp; Gross, B. L. From forest to field: perennial fruit crop domestication. Am. J. Bot. 98, 1389–1414 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2195">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Petit, R. J. &amp; Hampe, A. Some evolutionary consequences of being a tree. Annu. Rev. Ecol. Evol. Syst. 37, 187–214 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2198">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Khan, M. A. &amp; Korban, S. Association mapping in forest trees and fruit crops. J. Exp. Bot. 63, 4045–4060 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2201">30</a></sup>, and it is complicated and time-consuming to obtain their double haploid or pure line materials of very low heterozygosity. The jujube genome was characterized as high in heterozygosity, high in density of SSRs and low in GC content. High level of heterozygosity will fragment the assembly of diploid genome sequenced only using the NGS technology or WGS strategy, whereas BAC-to-BAC strategy could avoid this problem via sequencing and assembling of each haploid BAC sequence. Due to the high SSR density and low GC content of jujube genome, some genomic regions were missed in the WGS-PCR sequencing. To retrieve such sequences, WGS-PCR-free library was constructed and sequenced, and 30 Mb sequences were added to the final assembly. The finished assembly covers 98.6% of the estimated jujube genome, &gt;98% of the <i>de novo</i> transcriptome sequence and 1,942 published ESTs, and it shows an overall identity of 98.5% with four randomly chosen BAC sequences. Consequently, this integrated strategy provides a good solution for assembling the complex jujube genome based on the most widely used NGS technology nowadays.</p><p>Our genomic and RNA-Seq analyses of the jujube offers some insights into the molecular mechanisms underlying the extreme accumulation of vitamin C and sugar in fruit, the distinct self-shoot-pruning system and the outstanding tolerance to abiotic/biotic stress, which are what the main breeding objectives of fruit trees. Compared with orange and kiwifruit, two well-known vitamin C-rich fruits with expansion and high expression of genes involved in the biosynthesis and recycling of vitamin C (refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2210">31</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 264 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2213">32</a>), respectively, the gene involved in both the biosynthesis and the recycling of vitamin C are enhanced in jujube (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>). This is the first report on such a kind of accumulation mechanism of vitamin C in fruit to our knowledge, which might elucidate why the vitamin C content of jujube is much higher than that in orange and kiwifruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 2</a>). This study indicates that the jujube would be a useful source of genes of fruit nutrition, self-shoot-pruning and stress tolerance for introduction into other fruit crops. To better understand and use the specific traits of jujube, more studies should be carried out integrating further genomic and transcriptome analysis, physiological analysis and field experiment.</p><p>The newly obtained high-quality genome sequence and gene information linked to its valuable biological features, a relatively small genome of 444 Mb similar to rice (466 Mb) and diploid inheritance with 12 pairs of chromosomes as in rice, coupled with its suitable biological properties including very short generation time, relatively small tree size, easy and quick flower bud differentiation, long flowering season, ease of vegetative propagation and cultivation, and a well-established <i>in vitro</i> regeneration system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Dai, L., Zhao, J. &amp; Liu, M. Tissue culture of chinese jujube using different explants. Acta Hort. 840, 293–296 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2229">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Zhou, R. &amp; Liu, M. Establishment of high-efficient in vitro leaf regeneration system in Chinese jujube. Acta Hort. Sin. 33, 625–628 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2232">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Qi, Y. &amp; Liu, M. Factors influencing young embryo culture of Chinese jujube. J.
