Fruit Sci. 11, 25–28 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2235">35</a></sup>, will enable the use of the jujube as a potential reference genomic system for deciduous fruit trees.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">WGS sequencing and BAC sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from <i>in vitro</i> grown <i>Z. jujuba</i> Mill. ‘Dongzao’ plantlets. Paired-end and mate-pair Illumina WGS libraries were constructed with multiple insert sizes (170 bp to 40 kb) according to the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina). For each short insert size (170, 250, 500 and 800 bp) library construction about 5 μg genomic DNA was used, and for large insert size (2, 5, 10, 20 and 40 Kb) library construction 20~60 μg DNA was used. Genomic DNA was fragmented, linked to adapters and extracted at specific size after agarose gel electrophoresis. For large insert size, first, fragments were biotin-labelled and circularized after size selection, sheared and enriched by magnetic beads (Invitrogen). Purified DNA was amplified by PCR, cloned to vector to yield 20 libraries for Illumina Hiseq 2000 sequencing.</p><p>We obtained 188.86 Gb raw WGS sequencing data. In the raw data filtering process, several heuristic rules were applied: (1) we removed reads with &gt;2% Ns or with poly-A structure; (2) we removed reads with ≥40% low quality bases for short insert size libraries and ≥60% for large insert size libraries; (3) we removed reads containing adapters; (4) we removed paired reads with mutual overlaps; (5) we removed PCR duplicates. After filtering, 109.88 Gb high-quality data were retained, representing 249.72 × genome coverage.</p><p>To overcome the key issue of high heterozygosity, high repeat content, we also constructed BAC libraries with insert fragment size of 120 kb length. A total of 21,504 BAC clones were randomly selected to extract plasmids. For each clone, unique index primer and adapter index were linked to fragment end, and a 500 bp insert size library was constructed and used for Illumina sequencing to a coverage depth of ~5.8 × . A pooling of 2,688 libraries (equal to 28 × 96-well plates) was sequenced in one lane, totally 21,504 libraries were sequenced in eight lanes to generate 177.17 Gb raw data for BAC sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms6315#MOESM587">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14"><i>De novo</i> genome assembly</h3><p>The short insert size library data were corrected by correct_error programme in the SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2277">11</a></sup> software package, which changed error bases in low frequency K-mers to form high frequency K-mers. The jujube genome was <i>de novo</i> assembled using a hierarchical assembly strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhang, G. et al. The oyster genome reveals stress adaptation and complexity of shell formation. Nature 490, 49–54 (2012)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2284">36</a></sup> along with WGS strategy. We assembled WGS sequencing data using SOAPdenovo-2.04 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2287">11</a>) software with K-mer value set as 87. The procedure included three steps: (1) construct de Bruijn graph using the short insert size data; (2) construct scaffold by aligning reads onto the contigs and (3) fill gaps by local assembly through mapping reads to flanking sequences of gaps. For BAC sequencing data, we split each BAC library data by index sequences in SOAPdenovo-2.04 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2290">11</a>) software to assemble each BAC clone. We combined the above WGS assembled sequences with the above assembled BAC sequences based on the overlap information by Rabbit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="You, M. et al. A heterozygous moth genome provides insights into herbivory and detoxification. Nat. Genet. 45, 220–225 (2013)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2295">37</a></sup>, and then used the paired-end relationships of the large insert size sequencing data to link the scaffolds/contigs into super-scaffold sequence using SSPACE-v1.1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Boetzer, M. et al. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2298">38</a>). We filled gaps by GapCloser v1.10 programme in the SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2302">11</a></sup> software package. To overcome the problems associated with low GC content of the jujube, PCR-free libraries were also constructed and sequenced.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">RAD-seq for genetic map construction</h3><p>The mapping population consisted of 105 inter-specific hybrids from a cross between <i>Z. jujuba</i> Mill. ‘JMS2’ and <i>Z. acidojujuba</i> Cheng et Liu ‘Xing 16’. Each individual in the F1 population was genotyped with RAD-seq. The paired-end RAD reads was mapped by SOAP2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: An improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966–1967 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2319">39</a>). Based on the alignment result, the RAD-based SNP calling was done by SOAPsnp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, R. et al. SNP detection for massively parallel whole-genome resequencing. Genome Res. 19, 1124–1132 (2009)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2323">40</a></sup>. Two heterozygous SNP alleles or one heterozygous and one homozygous SNP allele between two parents were treated as potential SNP markers if the following criteria were satisfied: parents:sequencing depth ≥10, base quality ≥25, copy number ≤1.5; progenies:sequencing depth ≥6, base quality ≥20, copy number ≤1.5. The ratio of marker segregation was calculated by <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup>-test. Markers showing significantly distorted segregation (<i>P</i>-value &lt; 0.01) were excluded from the map construction. The double pseudo-test cross strategy was applied<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Grattapaglia, D. &amp; Sederoff, R. R. Genetic linkage maps of Eucalyptus grandis and Eucalyptus urophylla using a pseudo-testcross mapping strategy and RAPD markers. Genetics 137, 1121–1137 (1994)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2335">41</a></sup>. Linkage analysis was performed for markers present at least 85% using JoinMap 4.0 software with CP population type codes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Van Ooijen, J. W. JoinMap:emoji: 4, Software for the Calculation of Genetic Linkage Maps in Experimental Populations (ed. Kyazma B. V.) (Wageningen, The Netrherlands, 2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2339">42</a></sup>. An logarithm (base 10) of odds (LOD) score of 6.0 was initially set as the linkage threshold for linkage group identification. Twelve linkage groups that had the same number of jujube chromosomes were formed at a LOD threshold of 6.0 and ordered using the regression mapping algorithm. Linkage between markers, recombination rates and map distances were calculated using the Kosambi mapping function and the regression mapping algorithm with a recombination frequency threshold of 0.5. The mapping position of SNP markers were first aligned to the scaffolds and scaffolds with only one SNP marker could be anchored but not oriented owing to a lack of markers. Finally, we scanned all SNP markers to construct the linkage groups and to anchor scaffolds to chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Repeat annotation</h3><p>Repetitive elements were identified by Repbase-based method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2351">43</a></sup> , <i>de novo</i> approach and TRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2358">44</a></sup> software. We searched for repeats using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker. &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2362">45</a></sup> and RepeatProteinMask<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Smit, A. F. A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker. &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2366">45</a></sup> software against Repbase database, identifying TEs at DNA and protein level. Also, we employed Piler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. Piler: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21, i152–i158 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2371">46</a></sup>, RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2375">47</a></sup>, and LTR-FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2379">48</a></sup> programmes to build the <i>de novo</i> repeat libraries and we ran RepeatMasker against the <i>de novo</i> library to find and classify the repeats.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene prediction</h3><p>We used homology-based and <i>de novo</i> methods, as well as RNA-seq data, to predict genes in the <i>Z. jujuba</i> genome. For homology-based gene prediction, protein sequences from six other plant species (<i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>P. persica</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>) were initially mapped onto the <i>Z. jujuba</i> genome using TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2426">49</a></sup> (<i>E</i>-value&lt;1e−5), and the homologous genome sequences were aligned against the matching proteins using GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2434">50</a></sup> for accurate spliced alignments. Next, we used the <i>de novo</i> gene prediction methods Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2441">51</a></sup> and GenScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2445">52</a></sup>. We then integrated the homologues and those from <i>de novo</i> approaches using GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Elsik, C. G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2452">53</a></sup> to produce a consensus gene set. In addition, we aligned all the RNA-seq reads to the reference genome using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2457">54</a></sup> and predicted the open reading frames (ORFs)s from the resulting data. Finally, we combined the GLEAN set with the gene models produced from RNA-seq to generate a high confidence gene set.</p><p>We did functional gene annotation by BlastP alignment to KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2464">55</a></sup>, SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2468">56</a></sup> and TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2472">56</a></sup> databases. Motifs and domains were determined by InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan--an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2476">57</a></sup> against protein databases such as ProDom, PRINTS, Pfam, SMART, PANTHER and PROSITE, and Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. The gene ontology consortium. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2480">58</a></sup> was obtained from the corresponding InterProScan entries.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene family clusters</h3><p>The protein-coding genes from seven Rosales species&amp;#8232;(<i>F. vesca</i>, <i>P. persica</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>M. domestica</i>, <i>P. bretschneideri</i> and <i>C. sativa</i>) and <i>A. thaliana</i> were downloaded from NCBI. The longest ORF was chosen to represent each gene, and ORF of genes encoding &lt;50 amino acids were filtered out. The OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2517">59</a></sup> method was then used to cluster all the genes into paralogous and orthologous groups on the BLASTP alignment with pairwise comparison strategy (<i>E</i>-value ≤1e−5).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Speciation time estimation</h3><p>The phylogenetic tree of eight species including jujube was constructed. The divergence time between jujube and seven other sequenced species of Rosales (<i>A. thaliana</i> as the outgroup) was estimated using MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Huelsenbeck, J.P. &amp; Ronquist, F. MRBAYES: Bayesian inference of phylogenetic trees. Bioinformatics 17, 754–755 (2001)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2535">60</a></sup> and the MCMCtree programme was implemented in the Phylogenetic Analysis by Maximum Likelihood (PAML)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2539">61</a></sup> software package. Calibration times was obtained from the TimeTree database (<a href="http://www.timetree.org/">http://www.timetree.org/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Transcriptome sequencing and analysis</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted from 15 samples (root, primary shoot, secondary shoot, MBS, bearing shoot, leaf, flower, young fruit, white mature fruit, half-red fruit and full red fruit of ‘Dongzao’, deciduous and lignified bearing shoots of ‘Dongzao’ and ‘Lizao’ (four samples)). The deciduous and lignified bearing shoots were sampled on 26 July 2013, the stage at which we could confirm the shoots are deciduous or lignified according to the morphological characters. RNA-seq libraries were constructed to sequence the transcriptome of each sample. RNA-seq libraries were sequenced on an Illumina Genome Analyzer platform. The resulting reads were aligned to the <i>Z. jujuba</i> genome sequences using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2561">54</a></sup>. After alignment, the count of mapped reads from each sample was derived and normalized to reads per kilobase of exon per million reads mapped for each predicted transcript using the Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2565">54</a></sup> package. In addition to conducting the analysis described above for the <i>Z. jujuba</i> transcriptome, we further assembled all reads from <i>Z. jujuba</i> using the Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2576">62</a></sup> package to evaluate the gene region coverage ratio.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Jujube-specific gene set analysis</h3><p>We obtained three types of specific gene set for jujube (expansion-related genes, positively selected genes and unique genes) from seven Rosales species (<i>Z. jujuba</i>, <i>F. vesca</i>, <i>P. persica</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>M. domestica</i> and <i>P. bretschneideri</i>).</p><p>To obtain the jujube unique gene families, we clustered gene families of the seven sequenced Rosales plant genomes. The genes from these genomes were collected and aligned to each other using BLASTP. Pairwise protein sequence similarity was used as the distance for clustering genes.</p><p>To detect genes evolving under positive selection in jujube, we used gene clustering of the seven Rosales species to identify 1,420 single-copy orthologous genes and used the optimized branch-site model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Zhang, J., Nielsen, R. &amp; Yang, Z. Evaluation of an improved branch-site likelihood method for detecting positive selection at the molecular level. Mol. Biol. Evol. 22, 2472–2479 (2005)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2616">63</a></sup> to identify 254 genes that are positively selected in <i>Z. jujuba</i>.</p><p>To detect expansion gene categories (SwissProt, KEGG and InterPro), we annotated the gene functions of the seven sequenced Rosales species genes and analyzed over-represented categories. Each domain of the three categories (SwissProt, KEGG and InterPro) was retrieved from the seven Rosales species, and examples in which the abundance differed between jujube and all six other species were identified by applying Fisher’s Exact test (significance was assumed if <i>P</i>&lt;0.05).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Fruit sugar analysis</h3><p>The experiment was designed as a complete randomized block with three replicates, and four plants were used in each replicate. All samples (each 0.5 kg fruits) were picked from <i>Z. jujuba</i> ‘Dongzao’ at four developing stages including young (60 days after fruit set), white mature (90 days after fruit set), half-red (105 days after fruit set) and full red (120 days after fruit set) in National Jujube Germplasm Repository, Taigu, China.</p><p>After drying overnight at 60 °C in the presence of silica gel, all samples were milled to powder. For each sample, 1 g of powder was transferred to a 50 ml volumetric flask and diluted to 40 ml with water of ultrapure grade. The flask was sonicated for 1 h at 80 °C. After cooling to room temperature, the mixture was diluted to the volume. The extracts were filtered through filter paper and a fraction of the filtrate was filtered through a 0.45 μm membrane filter. An HPLC system (Agilent 1200 HPLC Series, Waldbronn, Germany) equipped with a quaternary pump system, a refractive index detector (G1362A) was used for sucrose, glucose and fructose analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Wilson, A. M., Work, T. M. &amp; Bushway, A. A. HPLC determination of fructose, glucose, and sucrose in potatoes. J. Food Sci. 46, 300–301 (1981)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2643">64</a></sup>. Total sugar was determined by the anthrone method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Yemm, E. W. &amp; Willis, A. J. The estimation of carbohydrates in plant extracts by anthrone. Biochem. J. 57, 508–514 (1954)." href="/articles/ncomms6315#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d13381e2647">65</a></sup>. Three replicates were done for each sample and each analysis as well.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec23" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec23-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec23">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec23-content"><p><b>Accession codes:</b> The Jujube genome data has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession code JREP00000000. Sequence reads of transcriptome sequencing have been deposited in NCBI sequence read archive (SRA) under the accession code SRP046073.</p><p><b>How to cite this article</b>: Liu, M.-J. <i>et al</i>. The complex jujube genome provides insights into fruit tree biology. <i>Nat. Commun.</i> 5:5315 doi: 10.1038/ncomms6315 (2014).</p></div></div></section>

Multi-gene analysis provides a well-supported phylogeny o" /><span class="c-article-references__counter">9</span><p class="c-article-references__text" id="ref-CR9">Zhang, S., Solitis, D. M.-J.L., J.Z. and Q.-L.C. coordinated the overall project. G.-C.L. and Y.C. directed sequencing data generation. Q.-L.C., Y.C., Y.-D.S. performed the assembly. M.-J.L., J.Z., Q.-L.C., Y.C., X.-F.L., Y.-L.W. and L.-L.Y. performed gene annotation, SNP analysis, transcriptome analysis and database management. M.-J.L., J.Z., G.-C.L., X.-F.L. and B.W. analyzed the genome evolution. Y.C., C.Z., G.-C.L., Y.M., X.-F.L., S.-G.L., R.-J.H. and X.-M.G. performed repetitive elements, genome anchor, gene synteny and evolutionary analyses. M.-J.L., J.-RW., J.Z., J.-B.J. and W.Y. provided the materials and constructed the genetic map. M.-J.L., J.Z., J.-RW., Z.-H.Z, P.L., L.D., Z.G.L., M.-J.Lin., J.X.,Y.-Y.C., Z.Y. and X.-C.S. performed sample preparation, fruit nutrition detection and data analysis. M.-J.L. and J.Z. wrote the manuscript with contributions from Q.-L.C., G.-C.L., Z.-H.Z, P.L. J.-R.W., G.Y., L.D., W.-J.W., X.-S.L. and Y.C. The manuscript was revised by M.-J.L., J.Z., G.Y., Z.W., Y.-Y.Z. and L.-H.L.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms6315/email/correspondent/c1/new">Meng-Jun Liu</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare no competing financial interests.</p>
